<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top:#aaa 1px solid;text-align:center;">Statutory Instruments</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Police_and_Criminal_Evidence_(Northern_Ireland)_Order_1989" title="Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989">Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="navbox-abovebelow" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:#aaa 1px solid; border-bottom: #aaa 1px solid;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Crime_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Crime in the United Kingdom">Crime</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Terrorism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Terrorism in the United Kingdom">Terrorism</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:Law enforcement in the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:Law enforcement in the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Law enforcement in the United Kingdom</b> is organised separately in each of the legal systems of the United Kingdom: <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> Most law enforcement is carried out by <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">police officers</a> serving in regional police services (known as <a href="/wiki/Territorial_police_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Territorial police forces">territorial police forces</a>) within one of those jurisdictions. These regional services are complemented by UK-wide agencies, such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Crime_Agency" title="National Crime Agency">National Crime Agency</a> and the national specialist units of certain territorial police forces, such as the <a href="/wiki/Specialist_Operations" title="Specialist Operations">Specialist Operations</a> directorate of the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>.
</p><p>Police officers are granted certain powers to enable them to execute their duties. Their primary duties are the protection of life and property, preservation of the peace, and prevention and detection of criminal offences.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In the British model of policing, officers exercise their powers to police with the implicit consent of the public. "Policing by consent" is the phrase used to describe this. It expresses that the legitimacy of policing in the eyes of the public is based upon a general consensus of support that follows from transparency about their powers, their integrity in exercising those powers and their accountability for doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of law enforcement in the United Kingdom">History of law enforcement in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg/220px-London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg/330px-London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg/440px-London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4202" data-file-height="2781" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_-_Columbia_Market_-_2237.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A police officer on lunchbreak</div></div></div>
<p>In the 18th century law enforcement and policing was organised by local communities based on watchmen and constables; the government was not directly involved in policing. The <a href="/wiki/City_of_Glasgow_Police" title="City of Glasgow Police">City of Glasgow Police</a>, the first professional police, was established following an Act of Parliament in 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-UKPWatchmen_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKPWatchmen-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> London had a population of nearly a million and a half people in the early 19th century but was policed by only 450 constables and 4,500 night watchmen.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The concept of professional policing was taken up by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a> when he became Home Secretary in 1822. Peel's <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Act_1829" title="Metropolitan Police Act 1829">Metropolitan Police Act 1829</a> established a full-time, professional and centrally-organised police force for the greater London area known as the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Legislation in the 1830s introduced policing in boroughs and many counties and, in the 1850s, policing was established nationally.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Peelian_Principles" class="mw-redirect" title="Peelian Principles">Peelian Principles</a> describe the philosophy that Sir Robert Peel developed to define an ethical police force. The principles traditionally ascribed to Peel state that:
</p>
<ul><li>Every police officer should be issued an <a href="/wiki/Warrant_card" title="Warrant card">identification number</a>, to assure accountability for his actions.</li>
<li>Whether the police are effective is not measured on the number of arrests, but on the lack of crime.</li>
<li>Above all else, an effective authority figure knows trust and accountability are paramount.  Hence, Peel's most often quoted principle that "The police are the public and the public are the police."</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peelian_Principles#The_Nine_Principles_of_Policing" class="mw-redirect" title="Peelian Principles">Nine principles of policing</a> were set out in the ‘General Instructions’ issued to every new police officer in the Metropolitan Police from 1829. However, the Home Office has suggested this list was more likely authored by Charles Rowan and Richard Mayne, the first and joint Commissioners of the Metropolitan Police.<sup id="cite_ref-UKP_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKP-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CIVITAS_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIVITAS-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The police historian Charles Reith explained in his <i>New Study of Police History</i> (1956)<sup id="cite_ref-UKP_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKP-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> that these principles constituted a philosophy of policing "unique in history and throughout the world because it derived not from fear but almost exclusively from public co-operation with the police, induced by them designedly by behaviour which secures and maintains for them the approval, respect and affection of the public".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This approach to policing became known as "policing by consent".<sup id="cite_ref-CIVITAS_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIVITAS-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first women police officers were employed during the First World War. Hull and Southampton were two of the first to employ women police, although Grantham was the first town to have a warranted policewoman.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1940s, police forces in the United Kingdom have been merged and modernised.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisdictions_and_territories">Jurisdictions and territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Jurisdictions and territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CrimeinUK.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/CrimeinUK.png/300px-CrimeinUK.png" width="300" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/CrimeinUK.png/450px-CrimeinUK.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/CrimeinUK.png/600px-CrimeinUK.png 2x" data-file-width="899" data-file-height="511" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CrimeinUK.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crime in England and Wales from the <a href="/wiki/Crime_Survey_for_England_and_Wales" title="Crime Survey for England and Wales">Crime Survey</a> (in 000s of crimes).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, every person has limited powers of <a href="/wiki/Arrest" title="Arrest">arrest</a> if they see a crime being committed: at <a href="/wiki/Scots_law#Common_law" title="Scots law">common law</a> in Scotland, and in England and Wales if the crime is <a href="/wiki/Indictable" class="mw-redirect" title="Indictable">indictable</a><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>&#160;– these are called "every person powers", commonly referred to as a "<a href="/wiki/Citizen%27s_arrest" title="Citizen&#39;s arrest">citizen's arrest</a>". In England and Wales, the vast majority of <a href="/wiki/Police_Oath#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Oath">attested</a> <a href="/wiki/Constables" class="mw-redirect" title="Constables">constables</a> enjoy full powers of arrest and search as granted by the <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Criminal_Evidence_Act_1984" title="Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984">Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984</a>. For the purposes of this legislation, "constables" is defined to mean all police officers, irrespective of <a href="/wiki/Police_ranks_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Police ranks of the United Kingdom">rank</a>. Although police officers have wide-ranging powers, they are still subject to the same laws as members of the public.  However, there are certain additional legal restrictions on police officers such as the illegality of taking industrial action and the ban on taking part in active politics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_law_enforcement_agency">Types of law enforcement agency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types of law enforcement agency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_law_enforcement_agencies_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="List of law enforcement agencies in the United Kingdom">List of law enforcement agencies in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>There are three general types of law enforcement agency in the United Kingdom, the first is mostly concerned with policing the general public and their activities and the others are concerned with policing of more specific matters:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Territorial police services</b>, which carry out the majority of policing. There are 45 <a href="/wiki/Territorial_police_force#United_Kingdom" title="Territorial police force">territorial police services</a> as of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> that cover a <a href="/wiki/Police_area" title="Police area">police area</a> (a particular region) and have an independent <a href="/wiki/Police_authority" title="Police authority">police authority</a> (England and Wales) or local authority or joint <a href="/wiki/Police_board" title="Police board">police board</a> (Scotland). The <a href="/wiki/Police_Act_1996" title="Police Act 1996">Police Act 1996</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Police_(Scotland)_Act_1967" title="Police (Scotland) Act 1967">Police (Scotland) Act 1967</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Police_(Northern_Ireland)_Act_2000" title="Police (Northern Ireland) Act 2000">Police (Northern Ireland) Act 2000</a>, prescribe a number of issues such as appointment of a <a href="/wiki/Chief_constable" title="Chief constable">chief constable</a>, jurisdiction and responsibilities for police forces in England and Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland respectively. Since the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Crime_Commissioner" class="mw-redirect" title="Police and Crime Commissioner">Police and Crime Commissioners</a> in November 2012 the police services in England and Wales no longer have a police authority and this work is done by the police and crime prevention commissioners who are elected every four years. Certain territorial police services have units within them which have a national role, such the Specialist Operations directorate of the Metropolitan Police.</li>
<li><b>National law enforcement agencies</b>, including the <a href="/wiki/National_Crime_Agency" title="National Crime Agency">National Crime Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a> (the latter which operates only in England, Scotland, and Wales). The <a href="/wiki/Serious_Organised_Crime_and_Police_Act_2005" title="Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005">Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005</a> refers to these as "<a href="/wiki/Special_police_force#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Special police force">special police forces</a>". The National Crime Agency operates across the United Kingdom (but in Scotland or Northern Ireland requires the agreement of the domestic prosecuting authority to do so) against organised crime and acts as the UK point of contact for foreign agencies. There are also non-police law enforcement agencies whose officers, while not police constables, enforce laws such as the <a href="/wiki/Serious_Fraud_Office_(United_Kingdom)" title="Serious Fraud Office (United Kingdom)">Serious Fraud Office</a> and <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HM Revenue and Customs</a>.</li>
<li><b>Miscellaneous police services</b>, mostly having their foundations in older legislation or <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>. These have a responsibility to police specific local areas or activities, such as ports and parks and before the passing of recent legislation such as the Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005 were often referred to as "special police services"; care must therefore be taken in interpreting historical use of that phrase. These constabularies are not within the scope of the legislation applicable to the previously mentioned organisations but can still be the subject of statutes applicable to, for example, docks, harbours or railways. Until the passing of <a href="/wiki/Railways_and_Transport_Safety_Act_2003" title="Railways and Transport Safety Act 2003">Railways and Transport Safety Act 2003</a>, the British Transport Police was such a force.</li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_law_enforcement_agencies_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="List of law enforcement agencies in the United Kingdom">list of law enforcement agencies in the United Kingdom</a> details the various services.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross-jurisdiction_powers">Cross-jurisdiction powers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cross-jurisdiction powers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Territorial police constables have certain powers of arrest in another one of the <a href="/wiki/Legal_jurisdictions_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal jurisdictions of the United Kingdom">UK's three legal jurisdictions</a> than they were attested in. There are four main provisions for them to do so&#160;– arrest with a warrant, arrest without a warrant for an offence committed in their home jurisdiction whilst in another jurisdiction, arrest without a warrant for an offence committed in another jurisdiction whilst in that jurisdiction, and mutual aid.  A fifth power of cross jurisdictional arrest is to be introduced by section 116 of the <a href="/wiki/Policing_and_Crime_Act_2017" title="Policing and Crime Act 2017">Policing and Crime Act 2017</a> which fills a loop hole in arrest powers in certain situations.  This new power will allow a constable from one jurisdiction to arrest without warrant a person suspected of an offence in another jurisdiction whilst in their home jurisdiction.  This power will be in relation to more serious offences as listed in the act.  The Act sets out how long the person can be detained in custody by the 'arresting force' in one jurisdiction until constables from the 'investigating force' in another jurisdiction can travel to re-arrest the person and deal accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  Although this power is on the statute book, it is yet to be commenced by statutory instrument as of January 2018.  Below is a summary of these five powers with a practical example due to the complicated nature of this area of law.  Note: this section applies to territorial police constables only, and not to others&#160;– except the British Transport Police, who also have <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police#Cross-border_powers" title="British Transport Police">certain cross-border powers</a> in addition to their natural powers.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_with_warrant">Arrest with warrant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Arrest with warrant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Certain warrants can be executed by constables even though they are outside their jurisdiction: <a href="/wiki/Arrest_warrant" title="Arrest warrant">arrest warrants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warrant_of_committal" title="Warrant of committal">warrants of committal</a> (all); and a warrant to arrest a witness (England, Wales or Northern Ireland); a warrant for committal, a warrant to imprison (or to apprehend and imprison), and a warrant to arrest a witness (Scotland).<sup id="cite_ref-S136_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S136-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> A warrant issued in one legal jurisdiction may be executed in either of the other two jurisdictions by a constable from either the jurisdiction where it was issued, or the jurisdiction where it is executed.<sup id="cite_ref-S136_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S136-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When executing a warrant issued in Scotland, the constable executing it shall have the same powers and duties, and the person arrested the same rights, as they would have had if execution had been in Scotland by a constable of a police force in Scotland. When executing a warrant issued in England &amp; Wales or Northern Ireland, a constable may use reasonable force and has specified search powers provided by section 139 of the Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_without_warrant:_offences_committed_in_home_jurisdiction">Arrest without warrant: offences committed in home jurisdiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Arrest without warrant: offences committed in home jurisdiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In very simple terms, this power allows constables of one jurisdiction to travel to another jurisdiction and arrest a person they suspect of committing an offence in their home jurisdiction.  For example, constables from <a href="/wiki/Kent_Police" title="Kent Police">Kent Police</a> investigating an offence of assault that occurred in their police area could travel over the border into Scotland and arrest the suspect without warrant found in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>.
</p><p>If a constable suspects that a person has committed or attempted to commit an offence in his legal jurisdiction, and that person is now in another jurisdiction, he may arrest (and in the case of a constable from Scotland, detain) them in that other jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-S137_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S137-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A constable from England &amp; Wales is subject to the same necessity tests for arrest (as under section 24 of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>) as he would be in England &amp; Wales, a constable from Scotland may arrest/detain if it would have been lawful to do so in Scotland and a constable from Northern Ireland is subject to the same necessity tests for arrest (as under Article 26 of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>) as he would be in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-S137_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S137-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A person arrested under the above powers:<sup id="cite_ref-S137_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S137-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>in Scotland, shall be taken to the nearest convenient designated police station or to a designated police station in a police area in which the offence is being investigated (England &amp; Wales or Northern Ireland),</li>
<li>in England or Wales, shall be taken to the nearest convenient police station (Scotland) or to a police station within a sheriffdom in which the offence is being investigated (Scotland), to the nearest convenient designated police station (Northern Ireland) or to a designated police station in which the offence is being investigated (Northern Ireland), or</li>
<li>in Northern Ireland, shall be taken either to the nearest convenient designated police station (England &amp; Wales) or to a designated police station in a police area in which the offence is being investigated (England &amp; Wales) or to the nearest convenient police station (Scotland) or to a police station within a sheriffdom in which the offence is being investigated (Scotland).</li></ul>
<p>A person <i>detained</i> under the above powers:<sup id="cite_ref-S137_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S137-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>in England or Wales, shall be taken to the nearest convenient police station (Scotland) or to a police station within a sheriffdom in which the offence is being investigated (Scotland), or to the nearest convenient designated police station (England or Wales), or</li>
<li>in Northern Ireland, shall be taken to the nearest convenient police station (Scotland) or to a police station within a sheriffdom in which the offence is being investigated (Scotland), or to the nearest convenient designated police station (Northern Ireland), as soon as reasonably practicable.</li></ul>
<p>Detention under these powers, which in Scotland normally lasts for twelve hours, can be extended for up to twenty four hours.<sup id="cite_ref-S138_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S138-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Arrest_without_warrant:_offences_committed_in_other_jurisdictions_.28known_as_reciprocal_powers_of_arrest.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_without_warrant:_offences_committed_in_other_jurisdictions_(known_as_reciprocal_powers_of_arrest)">Arrest without warrant: offences committed in other jurisdictions (known as reciprocal powers of arrest)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arrest without warrant: offences committed in other jurisdictions (known as reciprocal powers of arrest)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In simple terms, this power gives a constable of one jurisdiction whilst in another jurisidiction the same power of arrest as a constable of the jurisidiction they are visiting.  As a practical example, if constables from <a href="/wiki/Police_Scotland" title="Police Scotland">Police Scotland</a> are over the border in Manchester on enquiries and come across a burglary in progress they can arrest the suspect on suspicion of burglary using the same arrest powers as a constable of England or Wales.
</p><p>A constable from one legal jurisdiction has, in the other jurisdictions, the same powers of arrest as a constable of that jurisdiction would have.<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A constable from England or Wales has:<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>in Scotland, the same power of arrest as a constable from Scotland</li>
<li>in Northern Ireland, the same power of arrest as a constable from Northern Ireland would have under Article 26 of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989 (necessity test).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>A constable from Scotland has:<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>in England and Wales, the same power of arrest as a constable from England or Wales would have under section 24 of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984 (necessity test).<sup id="cite_ref-section_24_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-section_24-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Northern Ireland, the same power of arrest as a constable from Northern Ireland would have under Article 26 of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989 (necessity test).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>A constable from Northern Ireland has:<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>in Scotland, the same power of arrest as a constable from Scotland</li>
<li>in England and Wales, the same power of arrest as a constable from England or Wales would have under section 24 of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984 (necessity test).<sup id="cite_ref-section_24_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-section_24-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>When a constable arrests a person in England &amp; Wales, the constable is subject to the requirements of section 28 (informing of arrest),<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> section 30 (taking to a designated police station)<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and section 32 (search on arrest).<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> When a constable arrests a person in Scotland, the arrested person shall have the same rights and the constable the same powers and duties as they would have were the constable a constable of a police force in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> When a constable arrests a person in Northern Ireland, the constable is subject to the requirements of Article 30 (informing of arrest),<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Article 32 (taking to a designated police station)<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and Article 34 (search on arrest).<sup id="cite_ref-S140_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S140-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_without_warrant_by_a_constable_in_his_home_jurisdiction_for_an_offence_committed_in_another_jurisdiction">Arrest without warrant by a constable in his home jurisdiction for an offence committed in another jurisdiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Arrest without warrant by a constable in his home jurisdiction for an offence committed in another jurisdiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This power will be introduced by the Policing and Crime Act 2017 which received Royal Assent on 31 January 2017.  However, the section of the act relating to this power awaits a statutory instrument to bring the power into active law.  This has yet to happen as of January 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  Until  the introduction of this power, there is an issue whereby a constable in his home jurisidiction can not arrest a person suspected of an offence in another jurisidiction without a warrant.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>  Practically this means that either a warrant has to be applied for, or constables from the investigating force have to travel to the jurisidiction where the person suspected was believed to be to arrest under the cross border powers set out above.  In urgent and serious cases this potentially causes several issues around delay and suspects evading capture.  This new power will allow a constable of one jurisdiction to arrest without warrant a person suspected of an offence in another jurisidiction whilst in their home jurisdiction.  The Act sets out which offences this power of arrest will apply to in each jurisdiction, and how long the person arrested can be kept in custody, with relevant authorities, by the 'arresting force' to allow sufficient time for officers from the 'investigating force' in another jurisidiction to travel and re-arrest the detained person for the purposes of their investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a practical example of this power shortly to be introduced, a murder is committed in Aberdeen, Scotland.  The suspect immediately travels south over the border into England and is found by constables of <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire_Police" title="West Yorkshire Police">West Yorkshire Police</a> in Leeds.  These constables will have a power of arrest in relation to the homicide that occurred in Scotland without the need to have a warrant.
</p>
<h4><span id="Other_situations_.28including_Mutual_Aid.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_situations_(including_Mutual_Aid)">Other situations (including Mutual Aid)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other situations (including Mutual Aid)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Police forces often support each other with large-scale operations, such as those that require specialist skills or expertise and those that require policing levels that the host-forces cannot provide. Referred to as mutual aid, constables loaned from one force to another have the powers and privileges of a constable of the host force.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Constables from the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a> who are on protection duties in Scotland or Northern Ireland have all the powers and privileges of a constable of the host police force.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> A constable who is taking a person to or from a <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">prison</a> retains all the powers, authority, protection and privileges of his office regardless of his location.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Regardless of where they are in the United Kingdom, a constable may arrest under section 41<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and may stop and search under section 43<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_Act_2000" title="Terrorism Act 2000">Terrorism Act 2000</a> on suspicion of terrorism (defined by section 40<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Powers_of_officers">Powers of officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Powers of officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_police_constables">Territorial police constables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Territorial police constables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG/220px-Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG/330px-Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG/440px-Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Merseyside_and_Metropolitan_Police_officers.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An officer of the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police</a> with an officer of <a href="/wiki/Merseyside_Police" title="Merseyside Police">Merseyside Police</a> during a Football match between <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Ham_United_F.C." title="West Ham United F.C.">West Ham</a> at <a href="/wiki/Goodison_Park" title="Goodison Park">Goodison Park</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_(Manchester,_England)_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_%28Manchester%2C_England%29_2.jpg/300px-Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_%28Manchester%2C_England%29_2.jpg" width="300" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_%28Manchester%2C_England%29_2.jpg/450px-Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_%28Manchester%2C_England%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_%28Manchester%2C_England%29_2.jpg/600px-Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_%28Manchester%2C_England%29_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="611" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greater_Manchester_Police_officers_in_Piccadilly_Gardens_(Manchester,_England)_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Police Constables and an Inspector of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester_Police" title="Greater Manchester Police">Greater Manchester Police</a> on the beat in <a href="/wiki/Manchester_city_centre" title="Manchester city centre">Manchester city centre</a> after the <a href="/wiki/2008_UEFA_Cup_Final_Riots" class="mw-redirect" title="2008 UEFA Cup Final Riots">2008 UEFA Cup Final Riots</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most police officers are members of <a href="/wiki/Territorial_police_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Territorial police forces">territorial police forces</a>. A person must make a declaration before taking up office as a constable and having any powers; although this is sometimes still known as the <a href="/wiki/Police_oath" title="Police oath">police oath</a>, and the process sometimes referred to as "swearing in", it now takes the form of an "attestation" (in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>) or a "declaration" (in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>). The process is carried out in the presence of a magistrate, and is usually followed by the issue of a warrant card. This grants the officer all the powers and privileges, duties and responsibilities of a constable in one of the three distinct legal systems - either England and Wales, Scotland or Northern Ireland, and the territorial waters of that country. The limited circumstances where their powers extend across the border are described in the section above.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_constables">Other constables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other constables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many constables who are not members of territorial police forces. The most notable are members of the three forces referred to as <a href="/wiki/Special_police_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Special police forces">special police forces</a>: the <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_Police" title="Ministry of Defence Police">Ministry of Defence Police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_Nuclear_Constabulary" title="Civil Nuclear Constabulary">Civil Nuclear Constabulary</a>. Such officers have the "powers and privileges of a constable" in matters relating to their work.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> BTP and MDP officers have additional jurisdiction where requested by a constable of another force, in which case they take on that constable's jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-statutelaw.gov.uk_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statutelaw.gov.uk-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Upon request from the <a href="/wiki/Chief_police_officer" title="Chief police officer">chief police officer</a> of a police force, members of one of the above three forces can be given the full powers of constables in the police area of the requesting force.<sup id="cite_ref-statutelaw.gov.uk_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statutelaw.gov.uk-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> This was used to supplement police numbers in the areas surrounding the <a href="/wiki/2005_G8_summit" class="mw-redirect" title="2005 G8 summit">2005 G8 summit</a> at Gleneagles.
</p><p>Many acts allow companies or councils to employ constables for a specific purpose. There are 10<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> companies whose employees are sworn in as constables under section 79 of the <a href="/wiki/Harbours,_Docks,_and_Piers_Clauses_Act_1847" class="mw-redirect" title="Harbours, Docks, and Piers Clauses Act 1847">Harbours, Docks, and Piers Clauses Act 1847</a>. As a result, they have the full powers of a constable on land owned by the harbour, dock, or port and at any place within one mile of any owned land. There are also forces created by specific legislation, such as the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Tilbury_Police" title="Port of Tilbury Police">Port of Tilbury Police</a> (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Port_of_London_Act_1968&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Port of London Act 1968 (page does not exist)">Port of London Act 1968</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mersey_Tunnels_Police" title="Mersey Tunnels Police">Mersey Tunnels Police</a> (County of Merseyside Act 1989) and the <a href="/wiki/Epping_Forest_Keepers" title="Epping Forest Keepers">Epping Forest Keepers</a> (Epping Forest Act 1878).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parks_Constables">Parks Constables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Parks Constables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Under Article 18 of the Ministry of Housing and Local Government Provisional Order Confirmation (Greater London Parks and Open Spaces) Act 1967, <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="London Borough Council">London Borough Councils</a> are allowed to swear in council officers as constables for "securing the observance of the provisions of all enactments relating to open spaces under their control or management and of bye-laws and regulations made thereunder". Local Authority Parks Constables have all the powers of a constable in relation byelaws regulations and all enactments relating to open spaces, Article 19 of the Act was repealed by section 26(1) of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984 (Local Acts) and the power of arrest for Parks Constables is now contained in Section 24 of P.A.C.E 1984, further amendments to Article 19 covering coming to the aid and assistance of such a constable or officer were repealed by SOCPA 2005, as this provision is already covered in P.A.C.E 1984 (Legal Counsel 2007,2012).
No enforcement agency with a power of arrest or detention can operate outside of the provisions of P.A.C.E 1984; therefore all local powers of arrest and detention were brought into line under section 26(1) P.A.C.E 1984.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_staff">Police staff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Police staff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Police forces employ staff who perform many functions to assist officers and support the smooth running of their police force. They do not hold the office of constable.
In England &amp; Wales, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_police_officer" title="Chief police officer">chief police officer</a> of a territorial police force may designate any person who is employed by the police authority maintaining that force, and is under the direction and control of that chief police officer, as one or more of the following:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Police_Community_Support_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Community Support Officer">Police Community Support Officer</a></li>
<li>Investigating Officer,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Custody_assistant" title="Custody assistant">detention officer</a>, or</li>
<li>Escort Officer.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>They have a range of powers given by the <a href="/wiki/Police_Reform_Act_2002" title="Police Reform Act 2002">Police Reform Act 2002</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and their chief police officer decides which of these powers they may use. Unlike a police constable, a PCSO only has powers when on duty and in uniform, and within the area policed by their respective force.
</p><p>Until 1991, parking enforcement was primarily conducted by police-employed <a href="/wiki/Traffic_warden" class="mw-redirect" title="Traffic warden">traffic wardens</a>. Since the passage of the Road Traffic Act 1991, <a href="/wiki/Decriminalised_parking_enforcement" title="Decriminalised parking enforcement">decriminalised parking enforcement</a> has enabled local authorities to take on this role and now very few forces still employ Police Traffic Wardens, these include the Metropolitan Police Service however they have combined the role with PCSOs as <i>Traffic Police Community Support Officers</i>.
</p><p>In Scotland, <a href="/wiki/Police_Custody_and_Security_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Custody and Security Officer">Police Custody and Security Officers</a> have powers similar to those of detention officers and escort officers in England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Similar powers are available in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accredited_Persons">Accredited Persons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Accredited Persons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chief_police_officer" title="Chief police officer">Chief police officers</a> of territorial police forces<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> (and the British Transport Police<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>) can also give limited powers<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> to people not employed by the police authority, under <a href="/wiki/Community_Safety_Accreditation_Scheme" class="mw-redirect" title="Community Safety Accreditation Scheme">Community Safety Accreditation Schemes</a>. A notable example are officers of the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_and_Operator_Services_Agency" title="Vehicle and Operator Services Agency">Vehicle and Operator Services Agency</a>, who have been given powers to stop vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> This practice has been criticised by the <a href="/wiki/Police_Federation_of_England_and_Wales" title="Police Federation of England and Wales">Police Federation</a> who described it as "half-baked".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Members_of_the_armed_forces">Members of the armed forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Members of the armed forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> only, members of <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Armed_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Armed Forces">Her Majesty's Armed Forces</a> have powers to stop people<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> or vehicles,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> arrest and detain people for three hours<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and enter buildings to keep the peace<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> or search for people who have been kidnapped.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Commissioned_officers" class="mw-redirect" title="Commissioned officers">commissioned officers</a> may close roads.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> They may use reasonable force when exercising these powers.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the Customs Management Act 1979, members of Her Majesty's Armed Forces may detain people if they believe they have committed an offence under the Customs &amp; Excise acts, and may seize goods if they believe they are liable to forfeiture under the same acts.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Service Police/Military Police</b> - Service police are not constables under UK law and do not have any constabulary powers over the general public, however they have the full range of policing power that constables possess when dealing service personnel or Civilians subject to Service Law drawing their powers from the Armed Forces Act 2006. However, the service police can utilise the powers, available to all persons in England and Wales, under Section 24(A) of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984; which allows any person to arrest any individual they have reasonable grounds to believe is committing, or has committed, an indictable offence, and that a constable is not available to perform the arrest.[6] They are allowed to use such force as is reasonable in the circumstance to achieve this.[7] A similar power of arrest exists under Scottish common law, but there is a requirement to use the minimum amount of force and for the Military Police officer to have directly witnessed the individual commit the act for which they are arresting them.  The Service Police regularly assist territorial police forces and patrol throughout the UK.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_persons">Other persons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other persons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some employees of <a href="/wiki/Local_authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Local authority">local authorities</a> have powers of entry relating to inspection of businesses under the <a href="/wiki/Sunday_Trading_Act_1994" title="Sunday Trading Act 1994">Sunday Trading Act 1994</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and powers to give <a href="/wiki/Fixed_Penalty_Notice" class="mw-redirect" title="Fixed Penalty Notice">Fixed Penalty Notices</a> for offences such as littering, graffiti or one of the wide-ranging offences in the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.statutelaw.gov.uk/legResults.aspx?LegType=All+Legislation&amp;title=Clean+Neighbourhoods+and+Environment+Act+2005.&amp;searchEnacted=0&amp;extentMatchOnly=0&amp;confersPower=0&amp;blanketAmendment=0&amp;TYPE=QS&amp;NavFrom=0&amp;activeTextDocId=1417210&amp;PageNumber=1&amp;SortAlpha=0">Clean Neighbourhoods and Environment Act 2005</a>. Such powers may be granted under local bylaws or acts of parliament. The Community Safety Accreditation Scheme also led to the proliferation of accredited neighbourhood warden schemes (and supporting enforcement functions) employed by several <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local authorities</a> (and sometimes social housing providers) across England and Wales, such as <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_City_Council" title="Nottingham City Council">Nottingham City Council</a>'s Community Protection Officers.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When carrying out an investigation, staff of the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Police_Complaints_Commission" title="Independent Police Complaints Commission">Independent Police Complaints Commission</a> have the powers and privileges of constables throughout England and Wales and territorial waters.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, staff of the <a href="/wiki/Police_Ombudsman_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Ombudsman for Northern Ireland">Police Ombudsman for Northern Ireland</a> have certain powers under the Police and Criminal Evidence (Application to the Police Ombudsman) Order (Northern Ireland) 2009<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Employees of the National Crime Agency can be granted<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> the powers of a constable,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Revenue and Customs officer<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and immigration officer.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>  These designations can be unconditional or conditional; time limited or limited to a specific operation.
</p><p>Employees of the <a href="/wiki/Border_Force" title="Border Force">Border Force</a> may be <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration Officer">Immigration Officers</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Customs_officer" title="Customs officer">customs officers</a> and hold certain powers of arrest, detention and search.
</p><p>In England, <a href="/wiki/Water_bailiff#England_and_Wales" title="Water bailiff">water bailiffs</a> employed by the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a> have powers in relation to enforcement of fishing regulations. Scottish <a href="/wiki/Water_bailiff#Scotland" title="Water bailiff">water bailiffs</a> have similar powers. There are also seven types of <a href="/wiki/Court_officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Court officer">court officer</a> - <a href="/wiki/Bailiff#Scotland" title="Bailiff">two</a> in Scotland and <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Enforcement agent">five</a> in England &amp; Wales, commonly referred to as "bailiffs", who can enforce court orders and, in some cases, have powers of arrest.
</p><p>In England <a href="/wiki/Traffic_officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Traffic officer">Traffic officers</a> are employed by the <a href="/wiki/Highways_England" title="Highways England">Highways England</a> and maintain traffic flow on <a href="/wiki/Trunk_road" title="Trunk road">trunk roads</a> and some bridges and tunnels. There are different types of traffic officer and they are appointed under separate Acts. They have limited powers to direct traffic and place <a href="/wiki/Road_signs" class="mw-redirect" title="Road signs">road signs</a>, close lanes of an active motorway, and stop vehicles if they believe them to be unroadworthy for the road and condition they are driving in.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wildlife_inspector#United_Kingdom" title="Wildlife inspector">Wildlife inspectors</a> have certain powers of entry and inspection in relation to wildlife and licenses relating to wildlife.
</p><p>Employees of <a href="/wiki/Local_Authority_Fire_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Local Authority Fire Service">public fire and rescue services</a> have <a href="/wiki/Powers_of_the_fire_service_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Powers of the fire service in the United Kingdom">extensive powers in the event of an emergency</a>, and more limited powers in other circumstances, such as fire investigations.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Prison_officer" title="Prison officer">Prison officers</a> have the powers, authority, protection, and privileges of a constable when acting as prison officers.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Civilian_Security_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Civilian Security Officer">Civilian Security Officers</a> (CSO) of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Security_Guard_Service" title="Northern Ireland Security Guard Service">Northern Ireland Security Guard Service</a> whilst on duty hold similar powers to those of a <a href="/wiki/Police_Constable#United_Kingdom_and_the_Commonwealth" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Constable">Police Constable</a>, as allowed by the Emergency Laws (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1947. A CSO has the powers of arrest under the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989 (PACE).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accountability">Accountability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Accountability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 22 November 2012, police authorities outside London were replaced by directly-elected <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Crime_Commissioner" class="mw-redirect" title="Police and Crime Commissioner">Police and Crime Commissioners</a>. In London the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_Police" title="City of London Police">City of London Police</a> continued to be overseen by <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_Corporation" title="City of London Corporation">City of London Corporation</a>, whilst the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a> has responsibility for the governance of the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a> is supervised by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Policing_Board" title="Northern Ireland Policing Board">Northern Ireland Policing Board</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Police_Scotland" title="Police Scotland">Police Scotland</a> is overseen by the Scottish Police Services Authority.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Nuclear_Constabulary" title="Civil Nuclear Constabulary">Civil Nuclear Constabulary</a> had their own police authority established in 2004. These forces operate across the United Kingdom and their responsibility is to the specific activities they were established to police.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg/220px-Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg/330px-Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg/440px-Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2744" data-file-height="1948" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Police.boat.at.poole.arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Police harbour patrol boat in <a href="/wiki/Poole_Harbour" title="Poole Harbour">Poole Harbour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Her_Majesty.27s_Inspectorates_of_Constabulary"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Her_Majesty's_Inspectorates_of_Constabulary">Her Majesty's Inspectorates of Constabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Her Majesty&#039;s Inspectorates of Constabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Inspectorate_of_Constabulary" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Inspectorate of Constabulary">Her Majesty's Inspectorates of Constabulary</a> (HMIC) are the official bodies responsible for the examination and assessment of police forces to ensure their requirements are met as intended.
</p><p>There are two similarly named organisations:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Her Majesty's Inspectorate of Constabulary and Fire &amp; Rescue Services</b> (HMICFRS) - this organisation is responsible to the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> for police forces in England and Wales. It also inspects, by invitation, various UK <a href="/wiki/Special_police_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Special police force">special police forces</a> and elements of the Service (Military) Police. Since 2004, HMIC has also had responsibility for examining <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HM Revenue and Customs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Serious_Organised_Crime_Agency" title="Serious Organised Crime Agency">Serious Organised Crime Agency</a>. Inspection services have been provided on a non-statutory basis for the <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a>.  Since July 2017 it has also become responsible for inspecting Fire &amp; Rescue Services in England.</li>
<li><b>Her Majesty's Inspectorate of Constabulary for Scotland</b> (commonly known as HMIC) - this organisation is responsible to the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> and examines Scotland's territorial police forces, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Crime_and_Drug_Enforcement_Agency" title="Scottish Crime and Drug Enforcement Agency">Scottish Crime and Drug Enforcement Agency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Criminal_Record_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Criminal Record Office">Scottish Criminal Record Office</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Police_College" title="Scottish Police College">Scottish Police College</a> and the Scottish Police Information Strategy.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crown_dependencies_and_overseas_territories">Crown dependencies and overseas territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Crown dependencies and overseas territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_overseas_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="British overseas territories">British overseas territories</a> have their own police forces, the majority of which use the British model. Because they are not part of the United Kingdom, they are not answerable to the British Government; instead they are organised by and are responsible to their own governments (an exception to this is the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Base_Areas_Police" title="Sovereign Base Areas Police">Sovereign Base Areas Police</a>; as the SBAs' existence is solely for the benefit of the British armed forces and do not have full overseas territory status, the SBA Police are responsible to the Ministry of Defence). Because they are based on the British model of policing, these police forces conform to the standards set out by the British government, which includes voluntarily submitting themselves to inspection by the HMIC. Their vehicles share similarities with the vehicles owned by forces based in the UK, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Battenburg_markings" title="Battenburg markings">Battenburg markings</a>.
</p><p><b>List of British Overseas Territories Police Services/Forces:</b>
</p><p>The fourteen British Overseas Territories are:<sup id="cite_ref-collections.europarchive.org_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collections.europarchive.org-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
