<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Map">Map</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Map">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Overseas.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/British_Overseas.png/750px-British_Overseas.png" width="750" height="620" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/British_Overseas.png/1125px-British_Overseas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/British_Overseas.png/1500px-British_Overseas.png 2x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="2288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Overseas.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF5050; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;British Overseas Territories</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #66FF66; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;United Kingdom</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #3399FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ranks">Ranks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ranks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Police_ranks_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Police ranks of the United Kingdom">Police ranks of the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>Throughout the United Kingdom, the rank structure of police forces is identical up to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Chief_Superintendent" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Superintendent">Chief Superintendent</a>. At higher ranks, structures are distinct within <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police Service</a> and the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_Police" title="City of London Police">City of London Police</a> have a series of <a href="/wiki/Borough_Commander" class="mw-redirect" title="Borough Commander">Commander</a> and <a href="/wiki/Police_commissioner" title="Police commissioner">Commissioner</a> ranks as their top ranks whereas other UK police forces have <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Chief_Constable" class="mw-redirect" title="Assistant Chief Constable">assistants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Chief_Constable" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Chief Constable">deputies</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Chief_Constable" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Constable">Chief Constable</a> as their top ranks. All <a href="/wiki/Police_commissioner" title="Police commissioner">Commissioners</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_Constable" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Constable">Chief Constables</a> are equal in rank.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Police_Community_Support_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Community Support Officer">Police Community Support Officers</a> (PCSOs) were introduced following the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Police_Reform_Act_2002" title="Police Reform Act 2002">Police Reform Act 2002</a>, although some have criticised these as for being a cheap alternative to fully trained police officers.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uniform_and_equipment">Uniform and equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Uniform and equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Uniforms_and_equipment_of_the_British_police" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniforms and equipment of the British police">Uniforms and equipment of the British police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Police_use_of_firearms_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Police use of firearms in the United Kingdom">Police use of firearms in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg/250px-Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg/375px-Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg/500px-Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bedfordshire-Police-car.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bedfordshire_Police" title="Bedfordshire Police">Bedfordshire Police</a> <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Astra" title="Vauxhall Astra">Vauxhall Astra</a> patrol car</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cumbria_police_car.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cumbria_police_car.JPG/250px-Cumbria_police_car.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cumbria_police_car.JPG/375px-Cumbria_police_car.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cumbria_police_car.JPG/500px-Cumbria_police_car.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cumbria_police_car.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Cumbria_Constabulary" title="Cumbria Constabulary">Cumbria Constabulary</a> <a href="/wiki/Ford_Focus" title="Ford Focus">Ford Focus</a></div></div></div>
<p>Uniforms, the issuing of <a href="/wiki/Firearms" class="mw-redirect" title="Firearms">firearms</a>, type of patrol cars, and other equipment, varies by force.  
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Custodian_helmet" title="Custodian helmet">Custodian helmet</a> which is synonymous with the "bobby on the beat" image is frequently worn by male officers in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> (and formerly in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>), while the equivalent for female officers is the "bowler" hat. The flat <a href="/wiki/Peaked_cap" title="Peaked cap">peaked cap</a> is worn by vehicle patrolmen and higher-ranking officers.<sup id="cite_ref-HFAQ_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HFAQ-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike police in most other developed countries, the vast majority of British police officers do not carry firearms on standard patrol; they carry <a href="/wiki/Club_(law_enforcement)" class="mw-redirect" title="Club (law enforcement)">Extendable "ASP" or fixed Monadnock PR-24 batons</a> and CS/PAVA spray.
</p><p>Every territorial force has a specialist <a href="/wiki/Firearms_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Firearms Unit">Firearms Unit</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-waldren_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waldren-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> which maintains <a href="/wiki/Armed_Response_Vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed Response Vehicle">Armed Response Vehicles</a> to respond to firearms-related emergency calls.  One territorial force (the <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a>) and two of the <a href="/wiki/Special_police_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Special police force">special police forces</a>, (the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Nuclear_Constabulary" title="Civil Nuclear Constabulary">Civil Nuclear Constabulary</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_Police" title="Ministry of Defence Police">Ministry of Defence Police</a>), are routinely armed.
</p><p>London's <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police Service</a> (MPS) firearms unit is the <a href="/wiki/Specialist_Firearms_Command" title="Specialist Firearms Command">Specialist Firearms Command</a> (SC019 - formerly SO19 and C019), but every force in the United Kingdom has firearms-trained officers available should the need arise.  Metropolitan and City of London Police operate with three officers per Armed Response Vehicle (ARV), composed of a driver, a navigator, and an observer who gathers information about the incident and liaises with other units. Other police forces carry two <a href="/wiki/Authorised_Firearms_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorised Firearms Officer">Authorised Firearms Officers</a> instead of three. Armed Police carry a combination of weapons, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch" title="Heckler &amp; Koch">Heckler &amp; Koch</a> <a href="/wiki/MP5" class="mw-redirect" title="MP5">MP5</a> carbines, <a href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch" title="Heckler &amp; Koch">Heckler &amp; Koch</a> <a href="/wiki/PSG1" class="mw-redirect" title="PSG1">PSG1</a> Sniper <a href="/wiki/Rifle" title="Rifle">rifles</a>, Heckler &amp; Koch Baton Guns (which fire <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bullet" title="Plastic bullet">baton rounds</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch_G36" title="Heckler &amp; Koch G36">Heckler &amp; Koch G36</a>Cs to a number of specialist weapons such as the <a href="/wiki/Remington_Arms" title="Remington Arms">Remington</a> pump-action <a href="/wiki/Shotgun" title="Shotgun">shotgun</a>. Marksmen in the <a href="/wiki/London_Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="London Metropolitan Police">London Metropolitan Police</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a> and other also use <a href="/wiki/Accuracy_International_Arctic_Warfare" title="Accuracy International Arctic Warfare">Accuracy International Arctic Warfare</a>. Since 2009 Tasers have also been issued to members of response teams for use when deadly force may not be warranted.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_training">Initial training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Initial training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="England_and_Wales">England and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: England and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are three curricula for new police constables, special constables and police community support officers:<sup id="cite_ref-:0_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Initial_Police_Learning_and_Development_Programme" title="Initial Police Learning and Development Programme">Initial Police Learning and Development Programme</a> (IPLDP) for police constables</li>
<li>Initial Learning for the Special Constabulary (IL4SC) for special constables</li>
<li>PCSO National Learning Programme for police community support officers</li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_Police_Learning_and_Development_Programme">Initial Police Learning and Development Programme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Initial Police Learning and Development Programme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Initial_Police_Learning_and_Development_Programme" title="Initial Police Learning and Development Programme">Initial Police Learning and Development Programme</a></div>
<p>Successful completion of the IPLDP over two years will result in a (mandatory) Diploma in Policing (Level 3 <a href="/wiki/Qualifications_and_Credit_Framework" title="Qualifications and Credit Framework">Qualifications and Credit Framework</a>) consisting of ten mandatory units.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_Learning_for_the_Special_Constabulary">Initial Learning for the Special Constabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Initial Learning for the Special Constabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Derived from the IPLDP and although not linked to a formal qualification as such; IL4SC requires the learning outcomes and National Occupational Standards (NOSs) are met in order to become compliant. This curriculum will bring an officer to the 'point of safe and lawful accompanied patrol'.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>  This course equates to roughly 3.5 weeks of direct learning.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="PCSO_National_Learning_Programme">PCSO National Learning Programme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: PCSO National Learning Programme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Successfully completion of the PCSO NLP over a period of six months to a year will result in a non-mandatory Certificate in Policing and this equates to 10 weeks of direct learning and consists of six mandatory units. Four of these units also feature within the IPLDP and being a QCF qualification, this can allow for officers wishing to become police officers for 'Recognition of Prior Learning' (RPL) and the transfer of such units to the IPLDP scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>All initial probationer training in Scotland is undertaken at the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Police_College" title="Scottish Police College">Scottish Police College</a> (or SPC) at <a href="/wiki/Tulliallan_Castle" title="Tulliallan Castle">Tulliallan Castle</a>. Recruits initially spend 12 weeks at the SPC before being posted to their divisions and over the next two years return to the SPC a number of times to complete examinations and fitness tests.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Training is composed of four distinct modules undertaken at various locations with some parts being delivered locally and some centrally at the SPC.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Training for Special Constables is delivered locally at seven locations throughout Scotland over a series of evenings and/or weekends. The training is split into two parts, with the first phase being delivered in a classroom environment before being sworn in as a Special Constable and the second phase is delivered after being sworn in. Upon successful completion of both parts of the training programme Special Constables are awarded a certificate of achievement and would be eligible to complete an abbreviated course at the Scottish Police College should they later wish to join the <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Service of Scotland">Police Service of Scotland</a> as a regular officer.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_of_police_forces">Organisation of police forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Organisation of police forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As all police forces are autonomous organisations there is much variation in organisation and nomenclature, however outlined below are the main strands of policing that makes up police forces:
</p>
<ul><li>All police forces have teams of officers who are responsible for general beat duties and response to emergency and non-emergency calls from the public. These officers are generally the most visible and will invariably be the first interface a member of the public has with police. In general terms these officers will normally patrol by vehicle (though also on foot or bicycle in urban areas). They will generally patrol a sub-division or whole division of a police force area or in the case of the Metropolitan Police Service, a borough. Nearly all police officers begin their careers in this area of policing, with some moving on to more specialist roles. The Metropolitan Police Service calls this area of policing 'Response Teams', whilst other forces use terms such as 'patrol', 'section' and other variations.</li>
<li>Most local areas or wards in the country have at least one police officer who is involved in trying to build links with the local community and resolve long-term problems. In London, the Metropolitan Police Service addresses this area of policing with Safer Neighbourhood Teams. This entails each political ward in London having a Police Sergeant, two police constables and a few PCSOs who are ring fenced to address problems and build community links in their respective wards. Other police forces have similar systems but can be named 'Area officers', 'Neighbourhood officers', 'Beat Constables' and a number of other variations.</li>
<li>Criminal Investigation Departments (CID) can be found in all police forces. Generally these officers deal with investigations of a more complex, serious nature, however this again can differ from force to force.  Most officers within this area are detectives. Depending on the force in question this area of policing can be further divided into a myriad of other specialist areas such as fraud.  Smaller forces tend to have detectives who deal with a wide range of varied investigations whereas detectives in larger forces can have a very specialist remit.</li>
<li>All police forces have specialist departments that deal with certain aspects of policing. Larger forces such as Greater Manchester Police, Strathclyde Police and West Midlands Police have many and varied departments and units such as traffic, firearms, marine, horse, tactical support all named differently depending on the force.  Smaller forces such Dyfed Powys Police and Warwickshire Police will have fewer specialists and will rely on cross training, such as firearms officers also being traffic trained officers. The Metropolitan Police, the largest force in the country, has a large number of specialist departments, some of which are unique to the Metropolitan Police due to policing the capital and its national responsibilities. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_Protection_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Diplomatic Protection Group">Diplomatic Protection Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counter_Terrorism_Command" title="Counter Terrorism Command">Counter Terrorism Command</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fixated_Threat_Assessment_Centre">Fixated Threat Assessment Centre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Fixated Threat Assessment Centre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fixated_Threat_Assessment_Centre" title="Fixated Threat Assessment Centre">Fixated Threat Assessment Centre</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fixated_Threat_Assessment_Centre" title="Fixated Threat Assessment Centre">Fixated Threat Assessment Centre</a> is a joint police/mental health unit set up in October 2006 by the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Health (United Kingdom)">Department of Health</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police Service</a> to identify and address those individuals considered to pose a threat to VIPs or the Royal Family.
<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> They may then be referred to local health services for further assessment and potential <a href="/wiki/Involuntary_commitment" title="Involuntary commitment">involuntary commitment</a>. In some cases, they may be detained by police under the <a href="/wiki/Mental_Health_Act_1983#Civil_sections" title="Mental Health Act 1983">section 136 powers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mental_Health_Act_1983" title="Mental Health Act 1983">Mental Health Act 1983</a> prior to referral.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UK_Visas_and_Immigration">UK Visas and Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: UK Visas and Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/UK_Visas_and_Immigration" title="UK Visas and Immigration">UK Visas and Immigration</a>, previously <a href="/wiki/UK_Border_Agency" title="UK Border Agency">UK Border Agency</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>As part of the wide-ranging review of the Home Office, the then Home Secretary, <a href="/wiki/John_Reid,_Baron_Reid_of_Cardowan" title="John Reid, Baron Reid of Cardowan">John Reid</a>, announced in July 2006 that all British immigration officers would be uniformed. On 1 April 2007 the <a href="/wiki/Border_and_Immigration_Agency" title="Border and Immigration Agency">Border and Immigration Agency</a> (BIA) was created and commenced operation. However, there were no police officers in the Agency, a matter that attracted considerable criticism when the Agency was established - agency officers have limited powers of arrest.  Further powers for designated officers within the Agency, including powers of detention pending the arrival of a police officer, were introduced by the UK Borders Act 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Government has effectively admitted the shortcomings of the Agency by making a number fundamental changes within a year of its commencement.  On 1 April 2008 the BIA became the <a href="/wiki/UK_Border_Agency" title="UK Border Agency">UK Border Agency</a> following a merger with <a href="/wiki/UKvisas" class="mw-redirect" title="UKvisas">UKvisas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_entry" title="Port of entry">port of entry</a> functions of <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HM Revenue and Customs</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, Jacqui Smith, announced that the UK Border Agency (UKBA) <i>"...will bring together the work of the Border and Immigration Agency, UK Visas and parts of <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HM Revenue and Customs</a> at the border, </i> [and] <i>will work closely with the police and other law enforcement agencies to improve border controls and security."</i><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within months of this, the <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> revealed (in a 16-page response to a report by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Carlile,_Baron_Carlile_of_Berriew" title="Alex Carlile, Baron Carlile of Berriew">Lord Carlile</a>, the independent reviewer of <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_Act_2006" title="Terrorism Act 2006">UK terrorism legislation</a>) that the Home Office will issue a <a href="/wiki/Green_Paper" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Paper">Green Paper</a> proposing to take forward proposals by the Association of Chief Police Officers (England &amp; Wales) for the establishment of a new 3,000-strong national border police force to work alongside the Agency.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Border_Force">Border Force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Border Force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following a major enquiry into the <a href="/wiki/UK_Border_Agency" title="UK Border Agency">UK Border Agency</a> that exposed significant flaws in the operation of border controls, the Home Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, announced in 2012 that the <a href="/wiki/Border_Force" title="Border Force">Border Force</a>, which is responsible for manning all points of entry into the United Kingdom, would be split from the control of the UKBA and become a separate organisation with direct accountability to ministers and a "law-enforcement ethos".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brian_Moore_(police_officer)" title="Brian Moore (police officer)">Brian Moore</a>, the former <a href="/wiki/Chief_Constable" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Constable">Chief Constable</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire_Police" title="Wiltshire Police">Wiltshire Police</a>, was appointed as the first head of the new UKBF.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overseas_police_forces_in_the_UK">Overseas police forces in the UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Overseas police forces in the UK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are certain instances where police forces of other nations operate in a limited degree in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Direction_centrale_de_la_police_aux_fronti%C3%A8res" title="Direction centrale de la police aux frontières">Police aux Frontières</a></i> or PAF (French Border Police), a division of the <i><a href="/wiki/French_National_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="French National Police">Police Nationale</a></i>, is permitted to operate in regard to <a href="/wiki/Eurostar" title="Eurostar">Eurostar</a> rail services through the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a>. This includes on <a href="/wiki/Eurostar" title="Eurostar">Eurostar</a> trains to London, within the international terminal at <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras_International" class="mw-redirect" title="St Pancras International">St Pancras Station</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Ebbsfleet_International_railway_station" title="Ebbsfleet International railway station">Ebbsfleet International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashford_International_railway_station" title="Ashford International railway station">Ashford International</a> railway stations, and at the <a href="/wiki/Eurotunnel_Folkestone_Terminal" title="Eurotunnel Folkestone Terminal">Cheriton Parc Le Shuttle</a> terminal (alongside <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General_of_Customs_and_Indirect_Taxes" title="Directorate-General of Customs and Indirect Taxes">French Customs</a> officials). The PAF also operate at Dover Ferry terminals. This arrangement is reciprocated to the <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/UK_Border_Agency" title="UK Border Agency">UK Border Agency</a>, and UK Customs Officers on Paris bound trains and within the terminal at <a href="/wiki/Gare_du_Nord" title="Gare du Nord">Paris Gare du Nord</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurotunnel_Calais_Terminal" title="Eurotunnel Calais Terminal">Coquelles (Le Shuttle)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gare_de_Lille-Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Gare de Lille-Europe">Gare de Lille-Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brussels-South_railway_station" title="Brussels-South railway station">Bruxelles-Midi/Brussel-Zuid</a> and the Calais, Dunkerque, and Boulogne ferry terminals.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The French police officers are not permitted to carry their firearms in the London Terminal; the firearms must be left on the train.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" title="Garda Síochána">Garda Síochána</a></i> (Irish Police), under an agreement between the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> and the United Kingdom, has the right, alongside the <a href="/wiki/Radiological_Protection_Institute_of_Ireland" title="Radiological Protection Institute of Ireland">Radiological Protection Institute of Ireland</a>, to carry out inspections of the <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Sellafield nuclear facility</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2006 a small number of officers from the <i><a href="/wiki/Policja" title="Policja">Policja</a></i> (Polish Police) were seconded to the <a href="/wiki/North_Wales_Police" title="North Wales Police">North Wales Police</a> to assist with the supervision of foreign (largely eastern European) truck traffic largely on <a href="/wiki/European_route_E22" title="European route E22">European route E22</a> (the <a href="/wiki/A55_road" title="A55 road">A55 road</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The Chief Constable of North Wales has publicly stated (November 2006) that he is considering directly recruiting a small number of officers from Poland to assist with policing the substantial population of Polish people that have migrated to his area since Poland's accession to the EU in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Military_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Police">Military Police</a> of forces present in the UK within the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Visiting_Forces_Act_1952" title="Visiting Forces Act 1952">Visiting Forces Act 1952</a> are permitted to travel to/from relevant premises in uniform and their (usually distinctive) vehicles will occasionally be seen. Their powers (including the carrying of firearms) are generally limited by that and other legislation to those necessary for the performance of duties related to their own forces and to those possessed by the general public.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_force_mergers">Police force mergers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Police force mergers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> there were <a href="/wiki/List_of_police_forces_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="List of police forces in the United Kingdom">45 territorial police forces in the UK</a>. The territorial English and Welsh police forces were created in their current form by amalgamations made by either the <a href="/wiki/Police_Act_1964" title="Police Act 1964">Police Act 1964</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1972" title="Local Government Act 1972">Local Government Act 1972</a>. Scotland has since merged its 8 territorial forces into one. Northern Ireland has always had a single police force since the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Partition of Ireland</a> in 1922.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland_2">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2013, the 8 territorial police forces in Scotland were merged into a single Scottish police force, named "The Police Service of Scotland", or colloquially <a href="/wiki/Police_Scotland" title="Police Scotland">Police Scotland</a>. Merging these forces had been first mooted in 2010, and was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Labour_Party" title="Scottish Labour Party">Scottish Labour Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Conservative Party">Scottish Conservative Party</a> ahead of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_election,_2011" title="Scottish Parliament election, 2011">2011 Scottish Parliament election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> After a consultation process,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> confirmed on 8 September 2011 that a single police service would be created in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish Government stated that "reform will safeguard frontline policing in communities by creating designated local senior officers for every council area with a statutory duty to work with councils to shape local services. Establishing a single service aims to ensure more equal access to national and specialist services and expertise such as major investigation teams and firearms teams, whenever and wherever they are needed."<sup id="cite_ref-scotland1_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotland1-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The Police and Fire Reform (Scotland) Bill was published in January 2012<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and was approved on 27 June 2012 after scrutiny in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-scotland1_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotland1-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The Bill received <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Fire_Reform_(Scotland)_Act_2012" title="Police and Fire Reform (Scotland) Act 2012">Police and Fire Reform (Scotland) Act 2012</a>. This created a force of approximately 17,000 police officers, the second largest in the United Kingdom after the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police</a> in London.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2015, following the transfer of police oversight powers to the Scottish Government,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> the Justice Secretary announced proposals to further unify policing in Scotland by merging the <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a>'s operations north of the border with Police Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> This proposal subsequently came in for significant criticism from both the rail industry and unions.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_mergers_for_England_and_Wales">Proposed mergers for England and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Proposed mergers for England and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1981 <a href="/wiki/James_Anderton" title="James Anderton">James Anderton</a>, Chief Constable of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester_Police" title="Greater Manchester Police">Greater Manchester Police</a>, called for the number of forces to be reduced to 9 in England (one for each <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">Region</a>) and 1 for Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> A 2004 proposal by the <a href="/wiki/Police_Superintendents%27_Association" title="Police Superintendents&#39; Association">Police Superintendents' Association</a> for the creation of a single national police force, similar to <a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" title="Garda Síochána">Garda Síochána na hÉireann</a> was objected to by the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Chief_Police_Officers" title="Association of Chief Police Officers">Association of Chief Police Officers</a>. The government did not accept the proposal at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2005 to 2006, the government considered merging several territorial police forces in England and Wales. The review only concerned policing in outside of Scotland, Northern Ireland and Greater London. Likewise, the major non-territorial forces (<a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_Nuclear_Constabulary" title="Civil Nuclear Constabulary">Civil Nuclear Constabulary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_Police" title="Ministry of Defence Police">Ministry of Defence Police</a>) are responsible to other government departments, and would not have been affected either. The primary argument for merging forces is that forces with 4,000 or more officers would perform better and could save costs.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The view was supported by <a href="/wiki/HM_Inspectorate_of_Constabulary" class="mw-redirect" title="HM Inspectorate of Constabulary">HM Inspectorate of Constabulary</a>, who said in September 2005 that the existing structure was "no longer working".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Merger proposals were announced by the Home Secretary in early 2006. They proposed reducing the number of police forces to less than 25, with Wales and several <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">Regions of England</a> having one force each.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>  The consultation period on this second batch of mergers started on 11 April 2006, and would have finished on 11 August, with a target of April 2008 for the mergers coming into effect.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 20 June 2006 the new Home Secretary, <a href="/wiki/John_Reid,_Baron_Reid_of_Cardowan" title="John Reid, Baron Reid of Cardowan">John Reid</a>, announced that the contested mergers would be delayed for further discussion.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The only merger agreed to was with <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_Constabulary" title="Lancashire Constabulary">Lancashire Constabulary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumbria_Constabulary" title="Cumbria Constabulary">Cumbria Constabulary</a>. On 12 July 2006, the Home Office confirmed that all the mergers were to be abandoned, with the entire proposal taken back for consultation.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Greater_London">Greater London</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Greater London">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Metofficer.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Metofficer.JPG/220px-Metofficer.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Metofficer.JPG/330px-Metofficer.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Metofficer.JPG/440px-Metofficer.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Metofficer.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a> officers on the beat in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a></div></div></div>
<p>One of the most common merger proposals is to merge the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_Police" title="City of London Police">City of London Police</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police</a>.
</p><p>The 2005-06 merger proposals had not included Greater London. This was due to two separate reviews of policing in the capital - the first was a review by the Department of Transport into the future role and function of the British Transport Police. The second was a review by the Attorney-General into national measures for combating fraud (the City of London Police is one of the major organisations for combating economic crime).<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Both the <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Police_of_the_Metropolis" title="Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis">Metropolitan Police Commissioner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Ian_Blair" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Ian Blair">Sir Ian Blair</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a>, stated that they would like to see a single police force in London, with the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police</a> also absorbing the functions of the <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Police" title="British Transport Police">British Transport Police</a> in London.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> However, the proposal to merge both the BTP and City forces with the Met caused significant criticism from several areas; the House of Commons Transport Select Committee severely criticised the idea of the Metropolitan Police taking over policing of the rail network in a report published on 16 May 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_Corporation" title="City of London Corporation">City of London Corporation</a> and several major financial institutions in <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">The City</a> made public their opposition to the City Police merging with the Met.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In a statement on 20 July 2006, the Transport Secretary announced that there would be no structural or operational changes to the British Transport Police, effectively ruling out any merger<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The interim report by the Attorney General's fraud review recognised the role taken by the City Police as the lead force in London and the South-East for tackling fraud, and made a recommendation that, should a national lead force be required, the City Police, with its expertise, would be an ideal candidate to take this role.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> This view was confirmed on the publication of the final report, which recommended that the City of London Police's Fraud Squad should be the national lead force in combatting fraud, to "act as a centre of excellence, disseminate best practice, give advice on complex inquiries in other regions, and assist with or direct the most complex of such investigations"<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Separate from the proposals raised by the Mayor of London and Metropolitan Police Commissioner was a plan by the government to reform policing in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Parks_of_London" title="Royal Parks of London">Royal Parks</a>. Since 1872 this had been the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Parks_Constabulary" title="Royal Parks Constabulary">Royal Parks Constabulary</a>. A report by former Metropolitan Police Assistant Commissioner Anthony Speed provided three options to reform the RPC, with the decision taken that it should be merged with the Metropolitan Police.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The Met took over responsibility for policing the Royal Parks on 1 April 2004 with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Parks_Operational_Command_Unit" title="Royal Parks Operational Command Unit">Royal Parks Operational Command Unit</a>. The full merger and abolition of the Royal Parks Constabulary took place in May 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2016, following <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_election,_2016" title="London mayoral election, 2016">his election</a>, the Mayor of London <a href="/wiki/Sadiq_Khan" title="Sadiq Khan">Sadiq Khan</a> ordered a review, led by <a href="/wiki/Toby_Harris,_Baron_Harris_of_Haringey" title="Toby Harris, Baron Harris of Haringey">Lord Harris</a>, of London's preparedness in the face of potential terror attacks. Amongst the recommendations, which were published in October 2016, was a revisiting of the idea of merging the Metropolitan Police, City of London Police and British Transport Police. In commenting, both the City Police and BTP cautioned against the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The police are funded both by central government and by local government.<sup id="cite_ref-parliament16_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parliament16-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Central government funding is calculated based by a formula, based on several population and socio-economic factors which are used to determine the expected cost of policing the area.<sup id="cite_ref-parliament16_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parliament16-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the 2017/18 fiscal year, the budget for local Police and Crime Commissioners to spend on police is £11 billion, with an extra £1.5 billion allocated to counter-terrorism and other special programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The combined funding will reduce from £12.3 billion in 2017/18 to £11.6 billion in 2020/21. <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Inspectorate_of_Constabulary" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Inspectorate of Constabulary">Her Majesty's Inspectorate of Constabulary</a> (HMIC) estimates that officer numbers will fall by around 2%.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Audit_Office_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Audit Office (United Kingdom)">National Audit Office</a>, funding was decreased between 2011 and 2016 by 22% in real terms<sup id="cite_ref-parliament16_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parliament16-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> and police officer numbers fell by 20,000 from 2010 to 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianrising_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianrising-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Funding levels stayed the same in real terms between 2015 and 2018, with a decrease in central government funding made up for by an increase in local government funding.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Increased spending in some areas such as counter-terrorism has been offset by decreased spending in other departments.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc2/3/2017_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2/3/2017-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, a report from the Inspectorate found that most police forces were providing a good service, though it noted that some aspects such as investigations and neighbourhood policing were being compromised by "rationing" and cutbacks.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc2/3/2017_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2/3/2017-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> A report from the Inspectorate in March 2018 had similar findings; it reported improvement in neighbourhood policing and highlighted issues with response policing.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Several current and former chief constables were raising concerns about whether the police can meet foreseeable challenges with current levels of funding.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc2/3/2017_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2/3/2017-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths_after_contact_with_the_police">Deaths after contact with the police</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Deaths after contact with the police">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The police service is sometimes criticised for incidents that result in deaths due to police firearms usage or in police custody, as well as the lack of competence and impartiality in investigations <i>(in England and Wales <b>only</b>)</i> by the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Police_Complaints_Commission" title="Independent Police Complaints Commission">Independent Police Complaints Commission</a> after these events.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecomomist_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecomomist-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> stated in 2009:
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 60px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Bad apples ... are seldom brought to justice: no policeman has ever been convicted of murder or manslaughter for a death following police contact, though there have been more than 400 such deaths in the past ten years alone. The IPCC is at best overworked and at worst does not deserve the “I” in its name.
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 60px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; line-height:normal; text-align: right;"><cite>—&#8201;<i>The Economist</i><sup id="cite_ref-Ecomomist_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecomomist-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the year 2011/12 there were 15 deaths in or following police custody. There were two fatal police shootings and 39 people died from apparent suicide following contact with the police.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the year 2012/13 there were 15 deaths in police custody. Nearly half of those who died had known mental health issue and four of those who died had been restrained by police officers. There were 64 deaths by apparent suicide within 48 hours of release from police custody.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the year 2013/14 there were 11 deaths in or following police custody. The number of people recorded as having apparently committed suicide within 48 hours of release from police custody was 68, a ten-year high.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the year 2014/15 there were 17 deaths in or following police custody. There was one fatal police shooting and there were 69 apparent suicides following custody.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_shootings">Controversial shootings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Controversial shootings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Police_use_of_firearms_in_the_United_Kingdom#Controversial_shootings" title="Police use of firearms in the United Kingdom">Police use of firearms in the United Kingdom §&#160;Controversial shootings</a></div>
<p>The policy under which police officers in England and Wales use firearms has resulted in controversy. Notorious examples include the <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Waldorf_shooting" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen Waldorf shooting">Stephen Waldorf shooting</a> in 1983, the deliberate fatal shootings of <a href="/wiki/Death_of_James_Ashley" title="Death of James Ashley">James Ashley</a> in 1998, <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Harry_Stanley" title="Death of Harry Stanley">Harry Stanley</a> in 1999, and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Charles_de_Menezes" class="mw-redirect" title="Jean Charles de Menezes">Jean Charles de Menezes</a> in 2005, and the accidental non-fatal shooting of <a href="/wiki/2_June_2006_London_terror_raid" class="mw-redirect" title="2 June 2006 London terror raid">Abdul Kahar</a> in 2006.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths_in_police_custody">Deaths in police custody</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Deaths in police custody">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/UK_deaths_in_custody" title="UK deaths in custody">UK deaths in custody</a></div>
<p>From 1990 to July 2012, 950 deaths occurred in police custody.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997/98, 69 people died in police custody or following contact with the police across England and Wales; 26 resulted from deliberate self-harm.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two defined categories of death in custody issued by the Home Office:<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Category A: This category also encompasses deaths of those under arrest who are held in temporary police accommodation or have been taken to hospital following arrest. It also includes those who die, following arrest, whilst in a police vehicle.
</p>
<ul><li>s/he has been taken to a police station after being arrested for an offence, or</li>
<li>s/he is arrested at a police station after attending voluntarily at the station or accompanying a Constable to it, and is detained there or is detained elsewhere in the charge of a constable, except that a person who is at a court after being charged is not in police detention for those purposes.</li></ul>
<p>Category B: Where the deceased was otherwise in the hands of the police or death resulted from the actions of a police officer in the purported execution of his duty.
</p>
<ul><li>when suspects are being interviewed by the police but have not been detained;</li>
<li>when persons are actively attempting to evade arrest;</li>
<li>when persons are stopped and searched or questioned by the police; and</li>
<li>when persons are in police vehicles (other than whilst in police detention).</li></ul>
<p>Hundreds of people kill themselves within 48 hours of being released from police custody.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues_since_1984">Issues since 1984</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Issues since 1984">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> in the 1970s, serious urban <a href="/wiki/Riot" title="Riot">riots</a> and the police role in controlling industrial disorder in the 1980s, and the changing nature of police procedure made police accountability and control a major <a href="/wiki/Political_football" title="Political football">political football</a> from the 1990s onwards.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984-1985)" class="mw-redirect" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984-1985)">coal miners' strike</a> (1984&#8211;1985) saw thousands of police from various forces deployed to maintain public order and to prevent intimidation of those continuing to work during a national strike by miners, frequently resulting in violent confrontation.</li>
<li>Undercover TV programmes, the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>'s 2003 <i>The Secret Policeman</i><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> <a href="/wiki/Dispatches_(TV_series)" title="Dispatches (TV series)">Dispatches</a> 2006 <i>Undercover Copper</i><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> raised questions of standards within UK police forces.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Racism">Racism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Racism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Macpherson Report coined the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Institutionalised_racism" class="mw-redirect" title="Institutionalised racism">institutionalised racism</a>" to describe policies and procedures that adversely affect persons from ethnic minority groups after the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Stephen_Lawrence" class="mw-redirect" title="Death of Stephen Lawrence">death of Stephen Lawrence</a>. Some commentators and academics have claimed that <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">political correctness</a> and excessive sensitivity to issues of race and class have reduced the effectiveness of the police force, not least for people living in deprived areas or members of minority groups themselves.
</p><p>In 2003, ten police officers from <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester_Police" title="Greater Manchester Police">Greater Manchester Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Wales_Police" title="North Wales Police">North Wales Police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cheshire_Constabulary" title="Cheshire Constabulary">Cheshire Constabulary</a> were forced to resign after a <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> documentary, <i>The Secret Policeman</i>, shown on 21 October, alleged racism among recruits at <a href="/wiki/Bruche_Police_National_Training_Centre" title="Bruche Police National Training Centre">Bruche Police National Training Centre</a> at <a href="/wiki/Warrington" title="Warrington">Warrington</a>. On 4 March 2005 the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/magazine/3210614.stm">BBC</a> noted that minor disciplinary action would be taken against twelve other officers (eleven from Greater Manchester Police and one from <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_Constabulary" title="Lancashire Constabulary">Lancashire Constabulary</a>) in connection with the programme, but that they would not lose their jobs. In November 2003, allegations were made that some police officers were members of the <a href="/wiki/British_National_Party" title="British National Party">British National Party</a>.
</p><p>
In June 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Police_of_the_Metropolis" title="Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis">Metropolitan Police Commissioner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Bernard_Hogan-Howe" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Bernard Hogan-Howe">Sir Bernard Hogan-Howe</a>, said there was "some justification" in claims that the Metropolitan Police Service is "institutionally racist":</p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I have always said if other people think we are institutionally racist then we are. It is no good me saying we are not and then saying you must believe me, it’s nonsense, if they believe that. I think it is a label but in some sense there is a truth there for some people … You're very much more likely to be stopped and searched if you're a young black man. I can't explain that fully. I can give you reasons but I can't fully explain it. So there is some justification ... I think in some ways society is institutionally racist. We see lack of representation in many fields, of which the police are one.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy">Privacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Privacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the beginning of 2005 it was announced that the <a href="/wiki/Police_Information_Technology_Organisation" title="Police Information Technology Organisation">Police Information Technology Organisation</a> (PITO) had signed an eight-year £122 m contract to introduce <a href="/wiki/Biometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Biometric">biometric</a> identification technology.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>  PITO are also planning to use <a href="/wiki/Closed-circuit_television" title="Closed-circuit television">CCTV</a> <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition systems</a> to identify known suspects; a future link to the proposed <a href="/wiki/National_Identity_Register" class="mw-redirect" title="National Identity Register">National Identity Register</a> has been suggested by some.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_speech">Freedom of speech</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Freedom of speech">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The police have sometimes been accused of infringing on <a href="/wiki/Free_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Free speech">free speech</a>. In December 2005, author Lynette Burrows was interviewed by police after expressing her opinion on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_5_Live" title="BBC Radio 5 Live">BBC Radio 5 Live</a> that <a href="/wiki/Homosexual" class="mw-redirect" title="Homosexual">homosexuals</a> should not be allowed to adopt children.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>  The following month, Sir <a href="/wiki/Iqbal_Sacranie" title="Iqbal Sacranie">Iqbal Sacranie</a> was investigated by police for stating the <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> view that homosexuality is a sin.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Photography_of_police">Photography of police</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Photography of police">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Counter-Terrorism_Act_2008#Photographs_of_police_officers_in_public_places" title="Counter-Terrorism Act 2008">Counter-Terrorism Act 2008 §&#160;Photographs of police officers in public places</a></div>
<p>Section 76 of the Counter-Terrorism Act 2008 came into force on 15 February 2009<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> making it an offence to elicit, attempt to elicit, or publish information "...of a kind likely to be useful to a person committing or preparing an act of terrorism" about:<sup id="cite_ref-S76_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S76-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> a member of <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Armed_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Armed Forces">Her Majesty's Armed Forces</a>; a <a href="/wiki/Constable" title="Constable">constable</a>, the <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">Security Service</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secret_Intelligence_Service" title="Secret Intelligence Service">Secret Intelligence Service</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Headquarters" title="Government Communications Headquarters">Government Communications Headquarters</a>. Any person found guilty faces 10 years imprisonment and an unlimited fine.<sup id="cite_ref-S76_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S76-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> It is a defence for a person charged with this offence to prove that they had a reasonable excuse for their action.<sup id="cite_ref-S76_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S76-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> It is not otherwise illegal to photograph or film a police officer in a public place per se. It must be noted however that any film or photography recorded whilst a constable is dealing with an incident may be seized as it becomes evidence under section 19 of Police &amp; Criminal Evidence Act 1984.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Policing_of_public_protests">Policing of public protests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Policing of public protests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In April 2009, a total of 145 complaints were made following clashes between police and protesters at the <a href="/wiki/2009_G-20_London_summit_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 G-20 London summit protests">G20 summit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Incidents including the death of 47-year-old <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Ian_Tomlinson" title="Death of Ian Tomlinson">Ian Tomlinson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> minutes after an alleged assault by a police officer,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> and a separate alleged assault on a woman by a police officer,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> has led to criticism of police tactics during protests.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> In response, Metropolitan Police Commissioner Sir Paul Stephenson asked Her Majesty's Chief Inspector of Constabulary (HMIC) to review policing tactics,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> including the practice of <a href="/wiki/Kettling" title="Kettling">kettling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_undercover_conduct">Police undercover conduct</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Police undercover conduct">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During 2010 and 2011, it emerged that, while engaged in <a href="/wiki/Covert_policing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Covert policing in the United Kingdom">covert infiltration of protest groups</a>, <a href="/wiki/Undercover" class="mw-redirect" title="Undercover">undercover</a> police officers had <a href="/wiki/UK_undercover_policing_relationships_scandal" title="UK undercover policing relationships scandal">entered into intimate relationships with a number of people</a> on false pretences and under assumed aliases, in some cases sharing a home, making plans for weddings, or fathering children, only to vanish after some years when their role was complete. In 2015 a public inquiry under a senior judge was announced. In November 2015 the Metropolitan Police published an unreserved apology in which it exonerated and apologized to those women who had been deceived and stated the methodology had constituted abuse and a "gross violation" with severely harmful effects, as part of a settlement of their cases. In 2016 new cases continued to come to light.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_sex_abuse_allegations">Police sex abuse allegations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Police sex abuse allegations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2016, allegations of serious <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a> were made against hundreds of police officers in England and Wales, with several forces in England and Wales receiving 436 allegations of abuse of power for sexual gain against 436 police officers, including 20 <a href="/wiki/Police_community_support_officer" title="Police community support officer">police community support officers</a> and eight staff in the two years to March 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-hundreds_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hundreds-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Mike Cunningham, inspector of constabulary and former chief constable of Staffordshire police said: “It's the most serious form of corruption. It is an exploitation of power where the guardian becomes an abuser. What can be worse than a guardian abusing the trust and confidence of an abused person? There can be no greater violation of public trust.”<sup id="cite_ref-hundreds_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hundreds-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit">Brexit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Brexit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If there is a no deal <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> the UK will lose access to important cross border databases of criminals, which may make it harder for the police to keep the public safe.  Police leaders warn of “a significant loss of operational capacity” if the UK leaves the EU without an agreement on policing.  The Association of Police and Crime Commissioner sent a letter to Home Secretary, Sajid Javid stressing the need for co-operation with European policing and justice organizations after March 2019.  The letter states 32 measures are currently used daily including the European Arrest Warrant, the Schengen Information System - a database giving alerts about individuals - and the European Criminal Records Information System.  The letter stresses the importance and mutual benefit of continued cooperation between the UK and Europe to face mutual threats.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
